A.1Some Linux commands 
As you are already familiar with it, the Linux terminal is a window (console) that allows the 
typing of commands to interact with the computer. In the Ubuntu l avor of Linux, a terminal 
is opened by hitting simultaneously the key sequence CTRL-ALT-T (other Linux systems has 
dif f erent sequences of keys). A brief list of Linux commands that are available in any Linux 
l avor is listed below (they also work in any Unix terminal like the Mac terminal) : 
cat: used to dump the contents of a i le (by default) to the terminal. It is invoked like cat 
file(s)name. 
cd: used to change between directories. It is invoked like cd directory-name. 
cp: used to make a copy of a i le to a i le with a new name or with the same name if the copy 
goes to a dif f erent directory where the copied i le resides. It is invoked like: 
cp filename new-filename 
cp file(s)name directory-filename-path 
cp filename directory-filename-path/new-filename. 
cp -r: used to make a copy of a directory. It is invoked like 
cp -r directory-name new-directory-name. 
chmod -R: used to change permissions to i les or directories. Its basic usage goes like chmod 
-R u+r file-or-directory-name. 
chown: used to change the owner of a i le or directory.Its basic usage goes like chown 
new-user file-or-directory-name. 
exit: used to close a terminal. It is invoked like exit. 
file: used to know the type of a i le. It is invoked like file filename. 
gzip: used to pack i les in the zip format. Its basic usage goes like zip file.zip file(s)name. 
ls: used to list the i les in a directory. Its basic usage goes like ls. 
locate: used to i nd i les in the system. It is invoked like locate filename. 
man: used to show on screen a basic help of a command. It is invoked like man command-name.

mkdir: used to create a directory. It is invoked like mkdir directory-name. 
more: used to show the contents of a i le to the terminal screen by screen (meaning that that 
a large i le it shows in the terminal in small pieces of the screen size. The space bar needs to 
be hitting to go between screens). It is invoked like more filename. 
mv: used to change a i le or directory name to a new one or to move a i le or directory to an 
existing directory. Its basic usage goes like 
mv filename new-filename-or-existing-directory-name. 
pwd: used to show the current directory path. It is invoked like pwd. 
rm: used to delete i les or non empty directories. Its basic usage goes like 
rm -rf file-or-directory-name. 
rmdir: used to delete directories. It is invoked like rmdir directory-name. 
unzip: used to unpack zip i les. An straight-forward computation in i nding the answer to our exercise of the wheat problem 
involves the typing on many terms that follow a pattern. The involved amount of typing is 
prone to error, leading to obtaining a wrong answer. Fortunately, after i nding our answer we 
showed ways to check the obtained answer was the right one. The fact that there is a pattern in 
the typed terms allows the natural introduction of repetitive computation in Python. Previous 
to that we will need to introduce the notion of Python list and Python relational operators. 
Then will be ready to present two ways of looping in Python: the for and the while loop. 
2.7.1Python List 
An important basic Python type object is named list, which is an object that can contain zero 
or more objects in an ordered way separated by comma and enclosed by square brackets: 
[obj0,obj1,Â·Â·Â· ,objn] 
Also, like any other Python object, a list can be assigned to a variable, and we will be doing 
so: milista = [obj0,obj1,Â·Â·Â· ,objn] 
Here, the list object to the right of the equal sign was assigned to the variable milista. The 
list elements are numbered left to right starting from zero, and the way to access any of them 
is via a pattern containing the name of the variable referencing the list followed by a set of 
squared brackets enclosing the number of the element we ant to list (following our example, the 
element n is listed like milista[n]). Another way to referencing the elements of a list is by 
using negative integers from ân to â1. Extra details operating with list can be found in the 
documentation [https://docs.python.org/3/tutorial/datastructures.html]. 
Since the only Python objects we know so far are numbers (or variables referencing assigned 
numbers), so in our i rst encounter with list objects they will contain whole numbers (but the 
elements of a Python list can be objects of any type). Take a look at the following IPython 
session illustrating the notion of list and how to work with them (in following this example you

Each topic we've touched upon has deeper nuances and specifics that were covered in our discussion.Some examples of how to use functional programming principles in PythonChatGPTCertainly! Functional programming is a paradigm where programs are constructed by applying and composing functions. Python, being a multi-paradigm language, supports both object-oriented and functional programming styles. Here are some core principles of functional programming and how you can use them in Python:Immutable Data:
Functional programming discourages changing-state and mutable data. While Python doesn't enforce immutability, you can emulate this principle:pythonCopy code# Using tuples which are immutable
data = (1, 2, 3)
First-Class and Higher-Order Functions:
Functions in Python are first-class, meaning they can be passed around as arguments, returned from other functions, and assigned to variables. Higher-order functions either take one or more functions as arguments or return a function.pythonCopy codedef apply_function(data, func):
    return [func(item) for item in data]

result = apply_function([1, 2, 3, 4], lambda x: x*x)
Pure Functions:
A function is pure if its output is solely determined by its input and it doesn't have any observable side effects.pythonCopy codedef add(x, y):
    return x + y  # Given the same inputs, will always return the same output and doesn't affect anything outside.
Map, Filter, and Reduce:
These are common higher-order functions used in functional programming. Python has built-in functions map() and filter(), and reduce() can be found in the functools module.pythonCopy codenumbers = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5]

# Using map to square the numbers
squares = list(map(lambda x: x*x, numbers))

# Using filter to get even numbers
evens = list(filter(lambda x: x % 2 == 0, numbers))

# Using reduce to get product of numbers
from functools import reduce
product = reduce((lambda x, y: x * y), numbers)
Recursion:
In functional programming, recursion is used as a primary control structure where a function calls itself.pythonCopy codedef factorial(n):
    if n == 1:
        return 1
    else:
        return n * factorial(n-1)
Function Composition:
Combining multiple functions to create a new function.pythonCopy codedef multiply_by_two(x):
    return x * 2

def add_three(x):
    return x + 3

def compose(func1, func2):
    return lambda x: func1(func2(x))

# Creates a new function that multiplies by 2 and then adds 3
new_func = compose(add_three, multiply_by_two)
result = new_func(4)  # Returns 11
While Python isn't a purely functional language like Haskell, these principles can be applied to write cleaner, more understandable, and more bug-resistant code.Ok could I get some more simplified examples.ChatGPTOf course! Filter:Filter items out of a list.pythonCopy codeevens = list(filter(lambda x: x % 2 == 0, numbers))

Recursion:A function calling itself.pythonCopy codedef countdown(n):
    if n == 0:
        return "Done!"
    else:
        print(n)
        return countdown(n-1)

countdown(3)  # Outputs 3, 2, 1
Function Composition:Combine simple functions to create a complex one.pythonCopy codedef double(x):
    return x * 2

def increment(x):
    return x + 1

def double_and_increment(x):
    return increment(double(x))

print(double_and_increment(2))  # Outputs 5

EBDF  (This position shall not be used)ChatGPTSure! Here's a Python function to convert a number to its Cistercian numeral representation in Unicode:pythonCopy codedef number_to_cistercian(n):
    # Validate input
    if not (0 <= n < 10000):
        return "Invalid number. Only numbers between 0 and 9999 are supported."

    # Define the base Unicode values for units, tens, hundreds, and thousands
    base_units = 0xEBA0
    base_tens = 0xEBB1
    base_hundreds = 0xEBC1
    base_thousands = 0xEBD1

    # Extract digits
    units = n % 10
    tens = (n // 10) % 10
    hundreds = (n // 100) % 10
    thousands = (n // 1000) % 10

    # Convert digits to their Cistercian numeral representation
    cistercian_units = chr(base_units + units) if units != 0 else ""
    cistercian_tens = chr(base_tens + tens - 1) if tens != 0 else ""
    cistercian_hundreds = chr(base_hundreds + hundreds - 1) if hundreds != 0 else ""
    cistercian_thousands = chr(base_thousands + thousands - 1) if thousands != 0 else ""

    # Combine and return the Cistercian representation
    return cistercian_units + cistercian_tens + cistercian_hundreds + cistercian_thousands

# Test
number = 1234
print(number_to_cistercian(number))  # Outputs the Cistercian numeral for 1234
This function works for numbers between 0 and 9999. When given a number, it extracts the unit, tens, hundreds, and thousands digits. It then maps these digits to their corresponding Cistercian numeral in Unicode.Ok that might work. I want to be able to convert the Unix time 1694719811 to 0016 9471 9811 and then into three Cistercian glyphsChatGPTTo convert the Unix time 1694719811 to the format 0016 9471 9811, you can use string formatting. After that, use the function I provided earlier to convert each of these three 4-digit numbers to Cistercian numerals.Here's how you can do it:pythonCopy codedef number_to_cistercian(n):
    # ... (The function remains the same)

def unix_time_to_cistercian(unix_time):
    # Convert Unix time to formatted string
    formatted_time = "{:04} {:04} {:04}".format(
        unix_time // 100000000, 
        (unix_time // 10000) % 10000, 
        unix_time % 10000
    )
    
    # Extract the three 4-digit numbers
    num1, num2, num3 = map(int, formatted_time.split())

    # Convert to Cistercian
    cistercian_num1 = number_to_cistercian(num1)
    cistercian_num2 = number_to_cistercian(num2)
    cistercian_num3 = number_to_cistercian(num3)

    return cistercian_num1, cistercian_num2, cistercian_num3

# Test
unix_time = 1694719811
c1, c2, c3 = unix_time_to_cistercian(unix_time)
print(c1, c2, c3)
This function will format the Unix time into the desired string format and then convert each of the three numbers into Cistercian numerals.A list of all of the topics we've discussed.2 / 2ChatGPTCertainly! Here's a brief overview and some examples for each:itertoolsThe itertools module provides a set of tools for creating iterators for efficient looping.Common functions include cycle(), repeat(), combinations(), permutations(), and groupby().pythonCopy codeimport itertools

# cycle through a list infinitely
cycled = itertools.cycle([1, 2, 3])
# print(next(cycled)) # 1
# print(next(cycled)) # 2 and so on...

# get all combinations of a list
combinations = list(itertools.combinations([1, 2, 3], 2))
# Output: [(1, 2), (1, 3), (2, 3)]

These are just a few examples. Each module provides a plethora of functions and tools that are very useful for a range of tasks. It's worth diving deep into the documentation for each to fully appreciate their capabilities.Coroutines via Enhanced GeneratorsChatGPTCoroutines are a generalization of generators. While a generator produces data, a coroutine can also consume data. This makes coroutines suitable for tasks such as cooperative multitasking, asynchronous I/O, and certain design patterns.Coroutines are introduced in Python through the yield keyword, much like generators. However, in coroutines, yield can be used as an expression, allowing data to be sent into the coroutine.Here's a basic introduction to coroutines via enhanced generators:Basic Coroutine:pythonCopy codedef simple_coroutine():
    print("Coroutine started!")
    x = yield
    print("Coroutine received:", x)
    
# Create a coroutine
coro = simple_coroutine()

# Start the coroutine
next(coro)  # Coroutine started!

# Send a value into the coroutine
coro.send(42)  # Coroutine received: 42
Coroutine with a Loop:pythonCopy codedef coroutine_with_loop():
    while True:
        x = yield
        print("Received:", x)

coro = coroutine_with_loop()
next(coro)

for i in range(3):
    coro.send(i)
Using yield to Produce and Consume Values:pythonCopy codedef produce_and_consume():
    sent_value = None
    while True:
        received = yield sent_value
        if received is not None:
            print("Received:", received)
            sent_value = f"Reply to {received}"

coro = produce_and_consume()
next(coro)

response = coro.send(10)
print("Coroutine replied:", response)

response = coro.send(20)
print("Coroutine replied:", response)
Closing a Coroutine:
Using the close() method, you can shut down a coroutine:pythonCopy codecoro = simple_coroutine()
next(coro)
coro.send(42)
coro.close()
Handling Exceptions in Coroutine:
You can use the throw() method to raise exceptions inside a coroutine:pythonCopy codedef coroutine_with_exception():
    try:
        while True:
            x = yield
            print("Received:", x)
    except ZeroDivisionError:
        print("Exception caught inside coroutine!")

coro = coroutine_with_exception()
next(coro)
coro.throw(ZeroDivisionError)
Coroutines in Python are extremely powerful, especially when combined with the asyncio module for asynchronous programming.Examples of bash (! And !!) Commands in vimChatGPTThe ! and !! commands in Vim allow you to execute external shell commands without leaving the Vim environment. Here's a breakdown of their usage::!<command> - Execute an external command:
This allows you to execute any external shell command right from within Vim.vimCopy code:!ls -l
This will run the ls -l command, and you'll see the output without exiting Vim.:!! - Repeat the last external command:
If you've just executed an external command and want to run it again, you can simply use :!!.vimCopy code:!ls
:!!
The :!! command will execute the ls command again.:<range>! <command> - Filter text through an external command:
This is a powerful feature in Vim that lets you send some (or all) of your text through an external program and replace the selected text with the output of that program.For example, let's say you have a list of numbers and you want to sort them:vimCopy code5

This will sort the entire file.Replacing a line with the output of a command:

This will replace the CURRENT DATE line with the output of the date command.Inserting the output of a command:
If you're in insert mode, you can insert the result of an external command with Ctrl-r followed by = then system('<your-command>') and finally press Enter.Remember, Vim has its own environment, so any changes you make to the environment (like setting environment variables) in one of these commands won't persist when that command finishes. If you want to make lasting changes to the environment, you'll need to exit Vim and make those changes in the shell.How to use chrontab and other schedulers in LinuxChatGPTIn Linux, cron is a daemon that allows users to schedule jobs (commands or scripts) to run periodically at fixed times, dates, or intervals. crontab is the command used to manage cron jobs for a user. Here's a brief overview of how to use cron and crontab:1. Crontab Syntax:A line in the crontab file represents a job and is defined by a cron expression followed by a shell command to execute. The cron expression has 5 fields:scssCopy code*     *     *   *    *        command to be executed
-     -     -   -    -
|     |     |   |    |
|     |     |   |    +----- day of the week (0 - 6) (Sunday=0 or 7)
|     |     |   +------- month (1 - 12)
|     |     +--------- day of the month (1 - 31)
|     +----------- hour (0 - 23)
+------------- min (0 - 59)
2. Crontab Commands:Edit Your Crontab File:bashCopy codecrontab -e
Display Your Current Crontab Entries:bashCopy codecrontab -l
Remove Your Current Crontab Entries:bashCopy codecrontab -r
3. Examples of Crontab Entries:Run script.sh every day at 3:30 AM:javascriptCopy code30 3 * * * /path/to/script.sh
Run script.sh every Monday at 3:30 AM:bashCopy code30 3 * * 1 /path/to/script.sh
Run script.sh every hour on the half-hour:javascriptCopy code30 * * * * /path/to/script.sh
4. Other Schedulers:at:
The at command is used to schedule a job to run once at a specified time in the future.To use at, first install it:bashCopy codesudo apt-get install at    # On Debian/Ubuntu
sudo yum install at        # On CentOS
Example: To execute a command at 4 PM:bashCopy codeecho "/path/to/script.sh" | at 4pm
anacron:
anacron is similar to cron but is used for scenarios where the machine might not be running all the time, like laptops. It ensures that scheduled jobs get executed even if the system was off when they were supposed to run.systemd timers:
Modern Linux systems with systemd can use systemd timers as an alternative to cron. systemd timers are paired with service units to execute commands.5. Best Practices:Always give the full path to any commands or scripts in a crontab.Consider redirecting the output of your cron jobs to a log file or to /dev/null if you don't want email notifications.Use a tool like crontab.guru to help create or verify cron timings.Remember, cron is a powerful tool, but with great power comes great responsibility. Always test new entries to ensure they work as expected.How does anacron work, does it turn the computer back on?ChatGPTanacron is designed to handle the running of periodic jobs (usually daily, weekly, and monthly jobs) that might not get executed if the system isn't running all the time. It's particularly useful for systems like laptops that aren't guaranteed to be powered on 24/7. Here's how anacron works:Job Definition: Jobs for anacron are defined in the /etc/anacrontab file.Execution Timing: Unlike cron, which schedules jobs based on exact times, anacron schedules jobs based on the number of days elapsed since the job was last run. For example, a daily job will run once every day, but the exact time it runs can vary.No Continuous Running Daemon: anacron doesn't run as a continuously active daemon. Instead, it's typically invoked by a cron or systemd job once a day (usually when the system starts). When invoked, anacron checks timestamps in the /var/spool/anacron directory to determine which jobs need to be run.Handling Missed Jobs: If a job is missed (e.g., because the system was turned off), anacron will execute the job the next time it runs, ensuring that periodic tasks are still carried out even if their scheduled execution time was missed.System Startup Execution: Many distributions have anacron configured to run at startup. This ensures that if a computer was off for several days and is then powered on, any missed daily, weekly, or monthly tasks get executed.Does it turn the computer back on? No, anacron does not have the capability to power on a computer. If the computer is off, anacron simply waits until the next time it's run (usually at the next system startup) to check and execute any missed jobs.In essence, while cron is great for systems that are always on (like servers), anacron is beneficial for systems that might have downtime (like personal laptops), ensuring that periodic maintenance tasks don't get skipped just because the system was off at a particular scheduled time.Ok that makes sense. More examples of scheduling in LinuxChatGPTCertainly! In Linux, there are several methods and tools available for scheduling tasks:cron: This is the classic job scheduler in Unix-like operating systems.Jobs are defined in a crontab, which is a simple text file.Each user can have their own crontab, and there's also a system-wide crontab.Jobs are scheduled using a specific format to indicate when they should run (minute, hour, day, etc.).Example:javascriptCopy code30 14 * * * /path/to/script.sh

incron: Similar to cron, but instead of time-based scheduling, it uses inotify system calls to monitor filesystem events like changes in directories or files. This allows you to run scripts or commands in response to filesystem changes.systemd timers: Modern Linux systems with systemd can use systemd timers as an alternative to cron.Timer units end with .timer and are linked with a service unit to execute.Can be set to run on a calendar schedule or after a specific delay.Example timer file (/etc/systemd/system/example.timer):csharpCopy code[Unit]
Description=Run example.service every day

[Install]

These are more commonly found in high-performance computing environments.Remember, the most appropriate tool depends on the specific use case. For example, for simple, regular tasks, cron might be best. But if you need to run tasks based on filesystem changes, incron would be more suitable. So, yes, many might choose Julia for tasks like numerical simulations, data analysis, and scientific computing.A program in Julia to make animations of swarm behaviorChatGPTCreating an animation of swarm behavior in Julia requires a combination of swarm logic and visualization tools. One of the popular packages in Julia for creating animations is Plots.jl with the GR.jl backend.Below is a simplified example of swarm behavior using the Boids algorithm in Julia. We will model the movement of agents (or "boids") based on three basic rules:Cohesion: Move towards the average position of neighbors.Alignment: Align direction with the average direction of neighbors.Separation: Avoid crowding neighbors.First, you'll want to install necessary packages:juliaCopy codeusing Pkg
Pkg.add("Plots")
Pkg.add("GR")
Here's a basic program:juliaCopy codeusing Plots
gr()

const WIDTH = 100
const HEIGHT = 100
const NUM_BOIDS = 100
const VIEW_RADIUS = 10.0
const SEPARATION_DISTANCE = 3.0
const MAX_SPEED = 2.0

mutable struct Boid
    position::Vector{Float64}
    velocity::Vector{Float64}
end

function initialize_boids(num_boids)
    return [Boid([rand()*WIDTH, rand()*HEIGHT], [(rand() - 0.5) * MAX_SPEED, (rand() - 0.5) * MAX_SPEED]) for _ = 1:num_boids]
end

function distance(a::Boid, b::Boid)
    return sqrt((a.position[1] - b.position[1])^2 + (a.position[2] - b.position[2])^2)
end

function update_boids!(boids)
    for boid in boids
        center = [0.0, 0.0]
        avg_velocity = [0.0, 0.0]
        num_neighbors = 0
        for other in boids
            if other != boid && distance(boid, other) < VIEW_RADIUS
                center .+= other.position
                avg_velocity .+= other.velocity
                num_neighbors += 1
                if distance(boid, other) < SEPARATION_DISTANCE
                    boid.velocity .-= (other.position - boid.position)
                end
            end
        end
        if num_neighbors > 0
            center ./= num_neighbors
            avg_velocity ./= num_neighbors
            boid.velocity .+= (center - boid.position) * 0.005
            boid.velocity .+= avg_velocity * 0.05
        end

        # Limit speed
        speed = sqrt(boid.velocity[1]^2 + boid.velocity[2]^2)
        if speed > MAX_SPEED
            boid.velocity ./= (speed / MAX_SPEED)
        end

        boid.position .+= boid.velocity

        # Boundary wrapping
        boid.position[1] = (boid.position[1] + WIDTH) % WIDTH
        boid.position[2] = (boid.position[2] + HEIGHT) % HEIGHT
    end
end

function visualize_boids(boids)
    x = [boid.position[1] for boid in boids]
    y = [boid.position[2] for boid in boids]
    plot(x, y, seriestype=:scatter, xlim=(0, WIDTH), ylim=(0, HEIGHT), legend=false)
end

# Main function to animate
function animate_swarm()
    boids = initialize_boids(NUM_BOIDS)
    anim = @animate for i=1:100
        update_boids!(boids)
        visualize_boids(boids)
    end
    gif(anim, "swarm.gif", fps = 10)
end

animate_swarm()
This code will create a GIF animation showing the swarm behavior. Adjust the constants at the top of the script to change the behavior and appearance of the swarm.Could we try this in Python jupyter notebook?ChatGPTAbsolutely. In a Python Jupyter notebook, we can use matplotlib for visualization.First, you'll need to install the necessary libraries, if you haven't:pythonCopy code!pip install numpy matplotlib
Next, here's a simplified swarm behavior simulation (using the Boids algorithm) that you can run in a Jupyter notebook:pythonCopy codeimport numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from matplotlib.animation import FuncAnimation

# Parameters
NUM_BOIDS = 100
WIDTH = 100
HEIGHT = 100
VIEW_RADIUS = 10.0
SEPARATION_DISTANCE = 3.0
MAX_SPEED = 2.0

class Boid:
    def __init__(self):
        self.position = np.array([np.random.rand()*WIDTH, np.random.rand()*HEIGHT])
        self.velocity = (np.random.rand(2) - 0.5) * MAX_SPEED

def distance(a, b):
    return np.linalg.norm(a.position - b.position)

def update_boids(boids):
    for boid in boids:
        center = np.array([0.0, 0.0])
        avg_velocity = np.array([0.0, 0.0])
        num_neighbors = 0
        for other in boids:
            if other != boid and distance(boid, other) < VIEW_RADIUS:
                center += other.position
                avg_velocity += other.velocity
                num_neighbors += 1
                if distance(boid, other) < SEPARATION_DISTANCE:
                    boid.velocity -= (other.position - boid.position)
                    
        if num_neighbors > 0:
            center /= num_neighbors
            avg_velocity /= num_neighbors
            boid.velocity += (center - boid.position) * 0.005
            boid.velocity += avg_velocity * 0.05

        speed = np.linalg.norm(boid.velocity)
        if speed > MAX_SPEED:
            boid.velocity /= (speed / MAX_SPEED)
            
        boid.position += boid.velocity

        # Boundary wrapping
        boid.position[0] = (boid.position[0] + WIDTH) % WIDTH
        boid.position[1] = (boid.position[1] + HEIGHT) % HEIGHT

    return boids

# Initialization
boids = [Boid() for _ in range(NUM_BOIDS)]

# Visualization in Jupyter Notebook
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(6, 6))
scatter = ax.scatter([b.position[0] for b in boids], [b.position[1] for b in boids])

def animate(frame):
    global boids
    boids = update_boids(boids)
    scatter.set_offsets([boid.position for boid in boids])
    return scatter,

ax.set_xlim(0, WIDTH)
ax.set_ylim(0, HEIGHT)
anim = FuncAnimation(fig, animate, frames=100, interval=100, blit=True)
plt.show()
This will create an animation showing the swarm behavior right inside your Jupyter notebook.Give an explanation of how it works or what it representsChatGPTCertainly! The code provided simulates a simple flocking behavior using a set of rules. It's inspired by the Boids algorithm, originally developed by Craig Reynolds in 1986, to simulate bird flocking or fish schooling behavior.Here's a breakdown of the code and the behavior it represents:Initialization:We create a class called Boid which has two attributes: position and velocity.We randomly initialize the position and velocity of each boid in the simulation.Flocking Rules:The algorithm's behavior emerges from three primary rules which are applied to each boid:a. Separation: Avoid crowding boids that are too close. If another boid is closer than a predefined distance (SEPARATION_DISTANCE), the boid will move away from it. This simulates creatures not wanting to overlap or run into each other.b. Alignment: Align with the average heading of nearby boids. In the code, this is achieved by calculating the average velocity of all nearby boids and adjusting the current boid's velocity slightly toward the average.c. Cohesion: Move toward the average position (center) of nearby boids. This makes boids want to stay together as a group. In the code, we calculate the average position of all nearby boids and adjust the current boid's velocity slightly toward that center.Boundary Wrapping:To prevent boids from disappearing off one edge of the visualization and never returning, we make the boundaries wrap. This means a boid exiting on the right will reappear on the left and vice versa (same for top and bottom).Visualization:Using matplotlib, we visualize the boids as points in a 2D space. The FuncAnimation function from matplotlib.animation is used to repeatedly update the positions of the boids on the plot, creating an animation effect.In the broader sense, the simulated behavior can be likened to how birds flock together, fish swim in schools, or large groups of animals move cohesively. The Boids algorithm, and variations of it, are commonly used in computer graphics to create lifelike crowd scenes in movies or to study natural swarm behaviors in scientific research.Some better titles for the conversationChatGPTOf course! WITHBOOTSTRAPPEDDIFFUSIONPRIOR 
Jingxiang Sun1â, Bo Zhang3â , Ruizhi Shao1, Lizhen Wang1, Wen Liu2, Zhenda Xie2, Yebin Liu1â  
1 Tsinghua University, 
2 DeepSeek AI, 
3 Independent Researcher 
ABSTRACT 
We present DreamCraft3D, a hierarchical 3D content generation method that pro- 
duces high-f i delity and coherent 3D objects. We tackle the problem by leveraging 
a 2D reference image to guide the stages of geometry sculpting and texture boost- 
ing. A central focus of this work is to address the consistency issue that existing 
works encounter. To sculpt geometries that render coherently, we perform score 
distillation sampling via a view-dependent diffusion model. This 3D prior, along- 
side several training strategies, prioritizes the geometry consistency but compro- 
mises the texture i delity. We further propose Bootstrapped Score Distillation to 
specif i cally boost the texture. We train a personalized diffusion model, Dream- 
booth, on the augmented renderings of the scene, imbuing it with 3D knowledge 
of the scene being optimized. The score distillation from this 3D-aware diffu- 
sion prior provides view-consistent guidance for the scene. Notably, through an 
alternating optimization of the diffusion prior and 3D scene representation, we 
achieve mutually reinforcing improvements: the optimized 3D scene aids in train- 
ing the scene-specif i c diffusion model, which offers increasingly view-consistent 
guidance for 3D optimization. The optimization is thus bootstrapped and leads to 
substantial texture boosting. With tailored 3D priors throughout the hierarchical 
generation, DreamCraft3D generates coherent 3D objects with photorealistic ren- 
derings, advancing the state-of-the-art in 3D content generation. Code available 
at https://github.com/deepseek-ai/DreamCraft3D. 
1INTRODUCTION 
The remarkable success of 2D generative modeling (Saharia et al., 2022; Ramesh et al., 2022; Rom- 
bach et al., 2022; Gu et al., 2022) has profoundly shaped the way that we create visual content. 
3D content creation, which is crucial for applications like games, movies and virtual reality, still 
presents a signif i cant challenge for deep generative networks. While 3D generative modeling has 
shown compelling results for certain categories (Wang et al., 2023a; Chan et al., 2022; Zhang et al., 
2023b), generating general 3D objects remains formidable due to the lack of extensive 3D data. 
Recent research effort has sought to leverage the guidance of pretrained text-to-image generative 
models (Poole et al., 2022; Lin et al., 2023; Tang et al., 2023) and showcases promising results. 
The idea of leveraging pretrained text-to-image (T2I) models for 3D generation is initially proposed 
by DreamFusion (Poole et al., 2022). A score distillation sampling (SDS) loss is enforced to opti- 
mize the 3D model such that its renderings at random viewpoints match the text-conditioned image 
distribution as interpreted by a powerful T2I diffusion model. DreamFusion inherits the imaginative 
power of 2D generative models and can yield highly creative 3D assets. To deal with the over- 
saturation and blurriness issues, recent works adopt stage-wise optimization strategies (Mildenhall 
et al., 2021) or propose improved 2D distillation loss (Wang et al., 2023b), which leads to an en- 
hancement in photo-realism. However, the majority of current research falls short of synthesizing 
complex content as achieved by 2D generative models. In addition, these works are often plagued 
with the âJanus issueâ, where 3D renderings that appear plausible individually show semantic and 
stylistic inconsistencies when examined holistically.ChatGPTThe abstract and introduction provided outline the concept and research behind DreamCraft3D, a novel method for 3D content generation.Key Points:DreamCraft3D:A method for hierarchical 3D content generation that can produce high-fidelity and coherent 3D objects.Uses a 2D reference image to guide the geometry sculpting and texture boosting processes.Addresses the consistency issue encountered by other existing works.Score Distillation Sampling:Leverages a view-dependent diffusion model for sculpting geometries that render coherently.Ensures geometric consistency but may compromise texture fidelity.Bootstrapped Score Distillation:Introduced to boost the texture.Uses a personalized diffusion model called Dreambooth, trained on augmented renderings to impart it with 3D knowledge of the scene.This provides view-consistent guidance for the 3D scene.Bootstrapping:Refers to the alternating optimization of the diffusion prior and 3D scene representation.The optimization assists in training the scene-specific diffusion model, which in turn provides increasingly view-consistent guidance for 3D optimization.Outcome:DreamCraft3D creates coherent 3D objects with photorealistic renderings, marking a significant advancement in 3D content generation.3D Generative Modeling Challenges:While 2D generative modeling has achieved significant success, 3D content creation remains challenging, particularly due to limited available 3D data.Recent efforts have looked to pretrained text-to-image generative models for guidance.DreamFusion:An earlier proposal that utilized pretrained text-to-image models for 3D generation.Used a score distillation sampling loss to optimize 3D models.Issues with Current Research:Existing methods often don't match the complexity achieved by 2D generative models.They face the "Janus issue," where 3D renderings that seem correct individually have inconsistencies when viewed as a whole.DreamCraft3D addresses these challenges with a unique approach to 3D generation, guided by 2D reference images and leveraging tailored diffusion models. The method aims to improve both the fidelity and consistency of generated 3D content.In this paper, we propose DreamCraft3D, an approach to produce complex 3D assets while main- 
taining holistic 3D consistency. Our approach explores the potential of hierarchical generation. We 
draw inspiration from the manual artistic process: an abstract concept is i rst solidif i ed into a 2D 
draft, followed by the sculpting of rough geometry, the ref i nement of the geometric details and the 
painting of high-f i delity textures. We adopt a similar approach, breaking down the challenging 3D 
generation into manageable steps. Starting with a high-quality 2D reference image generated from 
a text prompt, we lift it into 3D via stages of geometry sculpting and texture boosting. Contrary to 
prior approaches, our work highlights how careful consideration of each stage can unleash the full 
potential of hierarchical generation, resulting in superior-quality 3D creation. 
The geometry sculpting stage aims to produce plausible and consistent 3D geometry from the 2D 
reference image. On top of using the SDS loss for novel views and photometric loss at the reference 
view, we introduce multiple strategies to promote geometric consistency. Foremost, we leverage 
an off-the-shelf viewpoint-conditioned image translation model, Zero-1-to-3 (Liu et al., 2023b), to 
model the distribution of novel views based on the reference image. Since this view-conditioned 
diffusion model is trained on diverse 3D data (Deitke et al., 2023), it provides a rich 3D prior that 
complements the 2D diffusion prior. Additionally, we i nd annealing the sampling timestep and pro- 
gressively enlarging training views are crucial to further improve coherency. During optimization, 
we transition from implicit surface representation (Wang et al., 2021) to mesh representation (Shen 
et al., 2021) for coarse-to-f i ne geometry ref i nement. Through these techniques, the geometry sculpt- 
ing stage produces sharp, detailed geometry while effectively suppressing most geometric artifacts. 
We further propose bootstrapped score distillation to substantially boost the texture. Existing view- 
conditioned diffusion models trained on limited 3D often struggle to match the i delity of modern 2D 
diffusion models. Instead, we i netune the diffusion model according to multi-view renderings of the 
3D instance being optimized. This personalized 3D-aware generative prior becomes instrumental in 
augmenting the 3D texture while ensuring view consistency. Importantly, we i nd that alternatively 
optimizing the generative prior and 3D representation leads to mutually reinforcing improvements. 
The diffusion model benef i ts from training on improved multi-view renderings, which in turn pro- 
vides superior guidance for optimizing the 3D texture. In contrast to prior works (Poole et al., 2022; 
Wang et al., 2023b) that distill from a i xed target distribution, we learn from a distribution that 
gradually evolves according to the optimization state. Through this âbootstrappingâ, our approach 
captures increasingly detailed texture while keeping the view consistency. 
As shown in Figure 1, our method is capable of producing creative 3D assets with intricate geomet- 
ric structures and realistic textures rendered coherently in 360â¦. Compared to optimization-based 
approaches (Poole et al., 2022; Lin et al., 2023), our method offers substantially improved texture 
and complexity. Meanwhile, compared to image-to-3D techniques (Tang et al., 2023; Qian et al., 
2023), our work excels at producing unprecedented realistic renderings in 360â¦renderings. These 
results suggest the strong potential of DreamCraft3D in enabling new creative possibilities in 3D 
content creation. 3D generative models have been intensively studied to generate 3D assets without tedious manual 
creation. Generative adversarial networks (GANs) (Chan et al., 2021; 2022; 2021; Xie et al., 2021; 
Zeng et al., 2022; Skorokhodov et al., 2023; Gao et al., 2022; Tang et al., 2022; Xie et al., 2021; Sun 
et al., 2023; 2022) have long been the prominent techniques in the i eld. Auto-regressive models 
have been explored (Sanghi et al., 2022; Mittal et al., 2022; Yan et al., 2022; Zhang et al., 2022; 
Yu et al., 2023), which learn the distribution of these 3D shapes conditioned on images or texts. 
Diffusion models (Wang et al., 2023a; Cheng et al., 2023; Li et al., 2023; Nam et al., 2022; Zhang 
et al., 2023a; Nichol et al., 2022; Jun & Nichol, 2023; Bautista et al., 2022; Gupta et al., 2023) have 
also shown signif i cant recent success in learning probabilistic mappings from text or images to 3D 
shape latent. However, these methods require 3D shapes or multi-view data for training, raising 
challenges when generating in-the-wild 3D assets due to the scarcity of diverse 3D data (Chang 
et al., 2015; Deitke et al., 2023; Wu et al., 2023) compared to 2D. 
3D-aware image generation aims to render images in novel views while offering some level of 
3D consistency. These works (Sargent et al., 2023; Skorokhodov et al., 2023; Xiang et al., 2023) 
often rely on a pretrained monocular depth prediction model to synthesize view-consistent images. 
3 
Whiletheyachievephoto-realisticrenderingsforcategoriesofImageNet, theyfallshortinproducing 
results in large views. There are a few recent attempts (Watson et al., 2022; Liu et al., 2023b) that 
train view-dependent diffusion models on 3D data and demonstrate promising novel view synthesis 
capability for open domain. However, these are inherently 2D models and cannot ensure perfect 
view consistency. 
Lifting 2D to 3D approaches improve a 3D scene representation by seeking guidance using estb- 
lished 2D text-image foundation models. Early works (Jain et al., 2022; Lee & Chang, 2022; Hong 
et al., 2022) utilize the pretrained CLIP (Radford et al., 2021) model to maximize the similarity 
between rendered images and text prompt. DreamFusion (Poole et al., 2022) and SJC (Wang et al., 
2022), on the other hand, propose to distill the score of image distribution from a pretrained diffusion 
model and demonstrate promising results. Recent works have sought to further enhance the texture 
realism via coarse-to-f i ne optimization (Lin et al., 2023; Chen et al., 2023), improved distillation 
loss (Wang et al., 2023b), shape guidance (Metzer et al., 2023) or lifting 2D image to 3D (Deng 
et al., 2023; Tang et al., 2023; Qian et al., 2023; Liu et al., 2023a). Recently, Raj et al. (2023) pro- 
poses to i netune a personalized diffusion model for 3D consistent generation. However, producing 
globally consistent 3D remains challenging. In this work, we meticulously design 3D priors through 
the whole hierarchical generation process, achieving unprecedented coherent 3D generation.ChatGPTThe "Related Work" section delves into previous research related to 3D generative models, highlighting the primary techniques and challenges encountered in the domain.Key Takeaways:3D Generative Models:Generative Adversarial Networks (GANs): GANs have been widely used in 3D generative models. Numerous references like Chan et al., Xie et al., Zeng et al., and Sun et al. underline the significance of GANs in this field.Auto-regressive Models: These models learn the distribution of 3D shapes based on images or texts. Works by Sanghi et al., Mittal et al., and Zhang et al. have explored this.Diffusion Models: They are probabilistic models that map text or images to 3D shape latents. Recent successes in this direction include research by Wang et al., Cheng et al., and Nam et al. One challenge with these models is the scarcity of diverse 3D data, making it difficult to generate "in-the-wild" 3D assets.3D-aware Image Generation:The objective is to render images in new perspectives with 3D consistency. This includes works by Sargent et al., Skorokhodov et al., and Xiang et al. These models use pretrained monocular depth prediction models for synthesis, achieving photo-realistic outcomes for specific categories. However, they face challenges in large view renderings.Some attempts, like those by Watson et al. and Liu et al., train view-dependent diffusion models on 3D data, showcasing potential in novel view synthesis. Still, they are essentially 2D models and can't guarantee perfect view consistency.Lifting 2D to 3D:These methods enhance a 3D scene representation using established 2D text-image foundation models.Initial efforts, such as those by Jain et al. and Hong et al., used the pretrained CLIP model to increase the likeness between rendered images and text prompts.Other approaches, like DreamFusion and SJC, employed score distillation from pretrained diffusion models, showcasing promising outcomes.Recent research has aimed to elevate texture realism through various techniques: coarse-to-fine optimization, improved distillation loss, shape guidance, and converting 2D images to 3D. Raj et al. fine-tuned a personalized diffusion model for 3D consistent generation.However, creating globally consistent 3D is still a challenge. The current work aims to overcome this by designing 3D priors throughout the entire hierarchical generation process, striving for unparalleled coherent 3D generation.In conclusion, while there have been several advancements in 3D generative models, achieving global 3D consistency remains an intricate challenge. The presented work seeks to address this by focusing on a meticulous hierarchical generation process.3PRELIMINARIES 
DreamFusion (Poole et al., 2022) achieves text-to-3D generation by utilizing a pretrained text-to- 
image diffusion model ÏµÏas an image prior to optimize the 3D representation parameterized by Î¸. 
The image x = g(Î¸), rendered at random viewpoints by a volumetric renderer, is expected to rep- 
resent a sample drawn from the text-conditioned image distribution p(x|y) modeled by a pretrained 
diffusion model. The diffusion model Ï is trained to predict the sampled noise ÏµÏ(xt;y,t) of the 
noisy image xtat the noise level t, conditioned on the text prompt y. A score distillation sam- 
pling (SDS) loss encourages the rendered images to match the distribution modeled by the diffusion 
model. Specif i cally, the SDS loss computes the gradient:

One way to improve the generation quality of a conditional diffusion model is to use the classif i er- 
free guidance (CFG) technique to steer the sampling slightly away from the unconditional sampling, 
i.e., ÏµÏ(xt;y,t)+ÏµÏ(xt;y,t)âÏµÏ(xt,t,â), where â represents the âemptyâ text prompt. Typically, 
the SDS loss requires a large CFG guidance weight for high-quality text-to-3D generation, yet this 
will bring side effects like over-saturation and over-smoothing (Poole et al., 2022). 
Recently, Wang et al. (2023b) proposed a variational score distillation (VSD) loss that is friendly 
to standard CFG guidance strength and better resolves unnatural textures. Instead of seeking a 
single data point, this approach regards the solution corresponding to a text prompt as a random 
variable. Specif i cally, VSD optimizes a distribution qÂµ(x0|y) of the possible 3D representations 
Âµ(Î¸|y) corresponding to the text y, to be closely aligned with the distribution def i ned by the diffusion 
timestep t = 0, p(x0|y), in terms of KL divergence: 
LVSD= DKL(qÂµ(x0|y)||p(x0|y)).(2) 
Wang et al. (2023b) further shows that this objective can be optimized by matching the score of 
noisy real images and that of noisy rendered images at each time t, so the gradient of LVSDis

âÎ¸ i 
.(3) 
Here, Ïµloraestimates the score of the rendered images using a LoRA (Low-rank adaptation) (Hu 
et al., 2021) model. The obtained variational distribution yields samples with high-f i delity textures. 
However, this loss is applied for texture enhancement and is helpless to the coarse geometry initially 
learned by SDS. Moreover, both the SDS and VSD attempt to distill from a i xed target 2D distri- 
bution which only assures per-view plausibility rather than a global 3D consistency. Consequently, 
they suffer from the same appearance and semantic shift issue that hampers the perceived 3D quality.

We propose a hierarchical pipeline for 3D content generation as illustrated in Figure 2. Our method 
i rst leverages a state-of-the-art text-to-image generative model to generate a high-quality 2D image 
from a text prompt. In this way, we can leverage the full power of state-of-the-art 2D diffusion mod- 
els to depict intricate visual semantics described in the text, retaining the creative freedom as 2D 
models. We then lift this image to 3D through cascaded stages of geometric sculpting and texture 
boosting. By decomposing the problem, we can apply specialized techniques at each stage. For 
geometry, we prioritize multi-view consistency and global 3D structure, allowing for some com- 
promise on detailed textures. With the geometry i xed, we then focus solely on optimizing realistic 
and coherent texture, for which we jointly learn a 3D-aware diffusion prior that bootstraps the 3D 
optimization. In the next, we elaborate on key design considerations for the two phases. 
4.1GEOMETRY SCULPTING 
At this stage, we aim to craft a 3D model such that it matches the appearance of the reference 
image Ë x at the same reference view while maintaining plausibility under different viewing angles. 
To achieve this, we encourage plausible image renderings for each randomly sampled view, recog- 
nizable by a pretrained diffusion model. This is achieved using the SDS loss LSDS, 
as def i ned in 
Equation 1. In order to effectively utilize guidance from the reference image, we penalize the pho- 
tometric difference between the rendered image and the reference via Lrgb= â¥ Ëm â (Ë x â g(Î¸;Ë c))â¥2

mask Ëm. Meanwhile, we implement the mask loss Lmask= â¥ Ëm â gm(Î¸;Ë c)â¥2to

the geometry prior inferred from the reference image, and enforce the consistency with the depth 
and normal map computed for the reference view. The corresponding depth and normal loss are

Despite these, maintaining consistent semantics and appearance across back-views remains a chal- 
lenge. Thus, we employ additional techniques to produce coherent, detailed geometry. 
3D-aware diffusion prior. We argue that the 3D optimization with per-view supervision alone is 
under-constrained. Hence, we utilize a view-conditioned diffusion model, Zero-1-to-3, which is 
trained on a large scale of 3D assets and offers an improved viewpoint awareness. The Zero-1-to-3 
is a i ne-tuned 2D diffusion model, which hallucinates the image in a relative camera pose c given 
the reference image Ë x. This 3D-aware model encodes richer 3D knowledge of the visual world and 
allows us to better extrapolate the views given a reference image. As such, we distill the probability 
density from this model and compute the gradient of a 3D-aware SDS loss for novel views: 
âÎ¸L3D-SDS(Ï,g(Î¸)) = Et,Ïµ[Ï(t)(ÏµÏ(xt; Ë x,c,y,t) â Ïµ)âx 
âÎ¸ ].(5) 
This loss effectively alleviates 3D consistency issues like Janus problem. However, the i netuning on 
limited categories of 3D data of inferior rendering quality impairs the diffusion modelâs generation 
capability, so the 3D-aware SDS loss alone is prone to induce deteriorated quality when lifting 
general images to 3D. Therefore, we employ a hybrid SDS loss, which incorporates both the 2D and 
3D diffusion priors simultaneously. Formally, this hybrid SDS loss provides the gradient as: 
âÎ¸Lhybrid(Ï,g(Î¸)) = âÎ¸LSDS(Ï,g(Î¸)) + ÂµâÎ¸L3D-SDS(Ï,g(Î¸)),(6) 
where we choose Âµ = 2 to emphasize the weight of the 3D diffusion prior. When computing LSDS, 
we adopt the DeepFloyd IF base model (Shonenkov et al., 2023), a diffusion model that operates at 
64 Ã 64 resolution pixel space and better captures coarse geometry. 
Progressive view training. However, directly deriving the free views in 360â¦may still result in 
geometric artifacts, such as extra chair legs, due to the ambiguity inherent in a single reference 
image. To solve this, we progressively enlarge the training views, gradually propagating the well- 
established geometry to 360â¦results. 
Diffusion timestep annealing. To align with the coarse-to-f i ne progression of 3D optimization, 
we adopt a diffusion timestep annealing strategy similar to Huang et al. (2023). At the start of 
optimization, we prioritize sampling larger diffusion timestep t from the range [0.7,0.85] when 
computing Equation 6 to provide the global structure. As training proceeds, we linearly anneal 
the t sampling range to [0.2,0.5] over hundreds of iterations. This annealing strategy allows the 
model to i rst establish a plausible global geometry in the early optimization phase before ref i ning 
the structural details. 
Detailed structural enhancement. We initially optimize an implicit surface representation with 
the corresponding volume rendering as in NeuS (Wang et al., 2021) to establish the coarse structure. 
Then, following Lin et al. (2023), we use this result to initialize a textured 3D mesh representation 
using a deformable tetrahedral grid (DMTet) (Shen et al., 2021) to facilitate high-resolution details. 
Moreover, this representation disentangles the learning of geometry and texture. Hence, at the end 
of this structural enhancement, we are able to solely ref i ne the texture and better preserve high- 
frequency details from the reference image.ChatGPT4.2 TEXTURE BOOSTINGAfter establishing the basic geometry, our pipeline transitions into the texture boosting phase, aiming to enhance the surface appearance of the 3D model to match the intricate visual semantics from the 2D image.Diffusion-based Texture Enhancement. We build upon the principle of diffusion models for texture enhancement. However, traditional 2D diffusion models are not directly suitable for 3D texturing. To address this, we train a new diffusion model named DreamBooth specifically designed for refining textures of 3D models.3D-aware Diffusion Prior. We fine-tune DreamBooth using multi-view renderings of our preliminary 3D models. This approach endows the model with a more robust understanding of 3D textures, ensuring the textures look consistent and coherent from multiple angles.Bootstrapped Distillation Sampling (LBSD). DreamBooth serves as an evolving diffusion prior, guiding the optimization of the 3D model's texture. This process involves a bootstrapped distillation sampling (LBSD) loss that incorporates feedback from DreamBooth, leading to the gradient:âÎ¸LBSD(Ï,g(Î¸))=Et,Ïµ[Ï(t)(ÏµÏ(xt;y,t)âÏµDreamBooth(xt;y,t))âxâÎ¸]\nabla\theta LBSD(Ï, g(\theta)) = Et,Ïµ[\omega(t)(ÏµÏ(xt;y,t) - \epsilon_{DreamBooth}(xt;y,t))\frac{\partial x}{\partial \theta}]âÎ¸LBSD(Ï,g(Î¸))=Et,Ïµ[Ï(t)(ÏµÏ(xt;y,t)âÏµDreamBoothâ(xt;y,t))âÎ¸âxâ]
where ÏµDreamBooth\epsilon_{DreamBooth}ÏµDreamBoothâ represents the noise sampled from DreamBooth.Iterative Refinement. The texture optimization process is iterative. After enhancing the texture using DreamBooth, we use the improved 3D model to generate new multi-view renderings and fine-tune DreamBooth. This cycle is repeated several times, each iteration yielding better and more coherent textures.Preserving Global Coherency. Ensuring that textures look consistent from every angle is a major challenge. To achieve this, we leverage information from various viewpoints during the optimization. The 3D-aware diffusion prior from the geometric sculpting phase is also beneficial in this stage, ensuring that textures align well with the 3D structure.Finalization. Once the iterative refinement process is complete, we finalize the 3D model by applying a series of post-processing techniques. These methods, such as mesh smoothing and UV mapping adjustments, ensure that the model is ready for various applications like 3D printing, virtual reality, and game development.5 CONCLUSIONDreamCraft3D presents a novel approach to generate 3D content from textual prompts. By intelligently leveraging the strengths of state-of-the-art 2D diffusion models and adapting them to the 3D domain, we achieve impressive results in both geometry and texture. The hierarchical pipeline, comprising geometric sculpting followed by texture boosting, ensures that the 3D models are both structurally sound and visually stunning. We believe that this method will pave the way for more creative and versatile applications of 3D content generation in the future.Quantitative comparison. To generate compelling 3D content that resembles the input image and 
consistently conveys semantics from various perspectives, we compare our technique with estab- 
lished baselines using a quantitative analysis. Our evaluation employed four metrics: LPIPS (Zhang 
et al., 2018) and PSNR for i delity measurement at the reference viewpoint; Contextual Dis- 
tance (Mechrez et al., 2018) for pixel-level congruence assessment; and CLIP score (Radford et al., 
2021) to estimate semantic coherence. Results depicted in Table 1 indicate that our approach signif- 
icantly surpasses the baselines in maintaining both texture consistency and i delity.

9 
5.3ANALYSIS 
The effect of 3D prior. In our paper we claim that the guidance offered by a 3D prior enhances the 
generation of globally plausible geometry. To ascertain its impact, an ablation study is conducted, 
where the 3D prior is deactivated. Figure 6 demonstrates that, in the absence of the 3D prior, the 
resultant character tends to exhibit the multifaceted Janus issue and suffers from irregular geometry. 
This observation underlines the signif i cance of a viewpoint-aware 3D prior in regulating a globally 
consistent shape. 
The effect of BSD. Figure 6 also presents an ablation study encompassing three texture optimiza- 
tion techniques: (1) BSD, (2) VSD, and (3) Score Distillation Sampling (SDS) with the traditional 
stable diffusion. The application of SDS has been observed to generate novel-view textures that are 
excessively smooth and over-saturated. In contrast, while VSD using standard stable diffusion can 
produce realistic textures, it yields a notably high inconsistency. In contrast, our proposed approach 
successfully generates textures that strike a balance between realism and consistency. 
Visualization of multiple stages. Figure 7 provides the visualization of the intermediate rendering 
results for each stage in our hierarchical pipeline. In the geometry sculpting stage, we convert Neus 
to DMTet to improve high-resolution geometry details. However, the improvement in texture is 
negligible. On the contrary, in the texture stage, we signif i cantly improve the texture quality with 
our proposed BSD. 
DreamBooth times. Figure 4 illustrates multi-view datasets for DreamBooth. The initial stage 
involves the introduction of substantial noise into each image to amplify detail richness, leading to 
inconsistent denoised images. However, as the textured mesh undergoes optimization, the produced 
renderings evolve towards increased consistency and photorealism, thereby enhancing the quality of 
the input dataset tailored for DreamBooth.

asset generation. This work introduces a meticulous geometry sculpting phase for producing plau- 
sible and coherent 3D geometries and a novel Bootstrapped Score Distillation strategy. The latter, 
by distilling from an optimizing 3D-aware diffusion prior and adapting to multi-view renderings of 
the instance being optimized, signif i cantly improves texture quality and consistency. DreamCraft3D 
produces high-f i delity 3D assets with compelling texture details and multi-view consistency. We be- 
lieve this work represents an important step towards democratizing 3D content creation and shows

It leverages the representations learned by deep networks to give a perceptual distance between two images.PSNR (Peak Signal-to-Noise Ratio): This is a standard metric for image quality assessment. Mathematically, it's defined as:

The goal is to make the student model perform as closely as possible to the teacher, but with the benefits of reduced size and computational requirements. The "score" part implies that the process is focused on matching the output scores (or probabilities) of the teacher model.Sampling: In the context of the provided information, this likely refers to the method by which new data samples (e.g., new viewpoints or textures for the 3D model) are generated based on the distillation process.In the context given, SDS with traditional stable diffusion is a method to optimize and generate textures, but it appears to have issues with producing overly smooth and saturated results.Classifier-free Guidance:A "classifier" in machine learning refers to an algorithm that determines the category an input belongs to. For instance, in image recognition, a classifier might decide whether a given picture is that of a cat, dog, or something else."Classifier-free guidance" suggests a process that doesn't rely on traditional classification techniques. Instead of depending on labeled data and making hard categorization decisions, this method likely relies on unsupervised or semi-supervised techniques to guide the generation or optimization process. In the context of 3D modeling, it would mean guiding the generation of the model without relying on a pre-defined set of categories or labels.Classifier-free methods can be beneficial when labeled data is scarce, expensive, or when the problem domain doesn't align neatly with predefined categories. They can leverage the underlying structure or patterns in the data without the constraints of rigid categorization.Both of these concepts are advanced techniques in the realm of machine learning and computer graphics. They aim to optimize and improve the process of generating or refining content, whether it's textures for 3D models or the models themselves.A.1IMPLEMENTATION DETAILS

diffusion model that regularizes the generated textures. Specif i cally, for a rendered image x from 
an arbitrary viewpoint, the visible section under the reference view is initially computed. This 
invariant portion during the generation process allows our inpainting model to i ll in the remaining 
segments. As these remaining parts adhere to geometric constraints, we integrate geometric normal 
information through a control net. Ultimately, this method permits us to enforce view consistency 
and generate realistic results using a control-net guided inpainting diffusion model. To preserve the 
high-quality generation output, we avoid utilizing this image directly as a loss against the rendered 
image. Instead, we subtly introduce it by constraining the norm of the latent variables: 
Lreg(Ï,g(Î¸)) = Î£(â¥E(x)â¥2â â¥E(xreg)â¥2)2.(8) 
Architectural details. In the Neus approach (Wang et al., 2021), we employ a single-layer Multi- 
Layer Perceptron (MLP) with 32 hidden units to simultaneously predict RGB color, volume den- 
sity, and normal. The inputs to this MLP are the concatenated feature vectors derived from multi- 
resolution hash encoding sampled with trilinear interpolation. To sparsify the Instant NGP repre- 
sentation, we implement density-based pruning every 10 iterations within an octree structure, as 
suggested by Magic3D (Lin et al., 2023). In our experiments, we use a bounding sphere with a 
radius of 2. For the density prediction, we utilise the softplus activation function and, following the 
approach of Poole et al. 2022, include an initial spatial density bias in order to encourage optimiza- 
tion in favor of the object-centric neural i eld. 
Camera and light augmentations. We follow Magic3D to add random augmentations to the cam- 
era and light sampling for rendering the shaded images. Differently, we sample the point light 
location such that the angular distance from the random camera center location (w.r.t. the origin) 
is sampled from Ïcamâ¼ U(0,Ï/3) with a random point light distance rcamâ¼ (7.5,10), and (b) 
we freeze the material augmentation unlike Dreamfusion and Magic3D, as we found it is bad for 
training convergence (c) In the coarse neus stage, we propose a i xed-random mixed camera pose 
strategy. Specif i cally, following the common practice of the current text-to-3D methods, random 
camera view sampling benef i ts scene optimization. However, Zero1-to-3 needs i xed camera intrin- 
sic parameters. Therefore, we let half of the GPUs sample the camera distance from U(3.2,3.5), 
and the Field-of-View from U(10,20), while the left GPUs are i xed to the default camera intrinsic. 
Time annealing. At the beginning of the geometry sculpting stage, We utilize a simple two-stage 
annealing of time step t in the score distillation objective. For the i rst several iterations we sample 
time steps t â¼ U(0.7,0.85) and then anneal into t â¼ U(0.2,0.50). We refer the readers to Prolif i c- 
Dreamer (Wang et al., 2023b). For the left iterations, we i x time steps to U(0.2,0.50). We also 
utilize a simple two-stage time annealing for the multi-view dataset generation, that is, for the i rst 
updating step, we select a time step t = 0.5 for all rendered images and then anneal it into t = 0.1 
along the later updating steps.ChatGPTThe provided text appears to be an excerpt from a research paper or technical documentation describing a novel 3D modeling method leveraging deep learning.
